  <raw>AIRCRAFT PLANT'S PLANS ARE STILL UP IN THE AIR



Don't believe everything you read, says Jim Host, spokesman for Gov. Ernie Fletcher. Louisville is still in the running for an Adam Aircraft plant that could employ 500.
On March 20 -- just two days after Fletcher went to Englewood, Colo., to discuss the project with Adam founder Rick Adam -- The Salt Lake Tribune quoted Adam as saying the plant was going to Ogden, Utah.

Ogden won, Adam said, because it has a skilled aerospace work force, and Utah is offering $10 million in tax incentives.
But Host said Adam had pledged to call Fletcher before he announced his decision, and Adam hasn't called. He also said Kentucky is preparing incentives to rival the Utah offer.
"It's not over. We're still in the game," Host added.
Stocks, bonds and basketball
One of the speakers at last week's Ethical Leadership Institute was Marjorie Kelly, editor and publisher of Business Ethics Magazine.
"If I only knew a little bit about basketball," the Minnesota resident told the audience, "I might know what's going on down here."
One of her Kentucky friends kept talking about the SEC, and "I thought she meant the Securities and Exchange Commission," Kelly said.
'Dear valued customer ...'
The folks at Peoples Bank &amp; Trust Company of Madison County may have sensed they were headed for trouble when they got a letter from the telephone company that began:
"Dear Valued Customer: We have some great news for you! We listened to your feedback, and Verizon SuperPages and SuperPages.com has improved our billing system ..."
That was in June.
In February, the bank got a "confirming copy" of an order for telephone book advertising that would cost $111 a month.
Trouble was, the bank hadn't ordered the advertising, and the statement had been mailed 14 days after the cancellation date for the order.
The bank protested and got nowhere, so it joined two other phone users with similar troubles and sued Verizon Directories Corp. on Feb. 24.
The suit was settled out of court on Wednesday.
Peoples Bank had gone from "valued customer" to plaintiff in eight short months.
Economics for dummies
There was a startling example of modern manufacturing economics at Dick's Sporting Goods recently.
Near the back of the store was a large stack of dumbbells. (No, not your spouse's relatives! The weights you lift to build muscles.)
Get this: The dumbbells were "Made in China" and being sold in Lexington for 44 cents a pound.
So it's cheaper to make hunks of metal in China and haul them halfway around the world than it is to make them in the USA near where they are sold. Amazing.
The Buzz is written by Jim Jordan with contributions from readers and the Herald-Leader staff. Reach Jordan at (859) 231-3242 or 1-800-950-6397, Ext. 3242, or jjordan1@herald-leader.com.</raw>
